Billy Franklin Graham (1918.11.7‚Äì2018.2.21)

Born in Charlotte, North Carolina, USA, Billy Graham was a renowned evangelical preacher. In 1936, he enrolled at Bob Jones University, a fundamentalist institution, but later transferred to Florida Bible Institute due to difficulties adapting to the strict fundamentalist environment. In 1940, he attended Wheaton College. Initially from a Presbyterian background, he shifted toward the Baptist tradition during his studies at Florida Bible Institute and was ordained as an evangelist in 1939 by the St. John‚Äôs Baptist Association.

In 1950, Graham founded the Billy Graham Evangelistic Association and began conducting evangelistic crusades worldwide, becoming an internationally recognized figure in evangelical circles. During the 1950s, his relatively open and inclusive attitude towards doctrinal interpretation and toward groups often rejected by fundamentalists, such as Catholics and Pentecostals, contributed to the emergence of the ‚ÄúNew Evangelical‚Äù movement. Although still within the conservative faith tradition, the New Evangelicals were comparatively more open and resisted anti-intellectualism. In 1956, Graham established Christianity Today, a publication representing the New Evangelical perspective (Martin 2020: 259-260; Dong Jiangyang 2004: 32).

Graham held a staunch anti-communist stance and became involved in Taiwan‚Äôs political and diplomatic affairs in the 1970s. In 1971, when the Republic of China withdrew from the United Nations, he played a mediatory role between Taiwan and Washington, engaging with figures such as Henry Kissinger and Richard Nixon. After the death of Chiang Kai-shek in 1975, Graham presided over a memorial service for him in Washington, D.C. Later that year, from late October to early November, he visited Taiwan to hold large-scale evangelistic meetings. These events were originally planned to coincide with Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs birthday, incorporating a celebration and tribute delegation to the Presidential Office. However, Chiang passed away in April.

Researcher Zeng Qingbao (2016: 236-239) argues that Taiwan‚Äôs pro-Kuomintang (KMT) ‚Äúparty-state Christians‚Äù leveraged Graham‚Äôs influence for both missionary and diplomatic purposes, linking his evangelistic crusades with Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs birthday celebrations. However, since Graham distanced himself from fundamentalism, theologically fundamentalist ‚Äúparty-state Christians‚Äù gradually grew apart from him.

1982Ôºö54-55ÔºõÈ´ò‰øäÊòé2001Ôºö271-275Ôºâ„ÄÇÈ´ò‰øäÊòéÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£Â±ÄÂã¢È©üËÆä‰∏î‰ªçÊòØÂ®ÅÊ¨äÊôÇÊúüÁöÑ1970Âπ¥‰ª£ÔºåÊï¢ÊñºÁÇ∫‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄÅÊ∞ë‰∏ª„ÄÅÂúãÂÆ∂ÂâçÈÄîÁ≠âË≠∞È°åËÄåËàáÂ®ÅÊ¨äÊîøÂ∫úÂ∞çÊäóÔºåËá≥‰ªä‰ªçË¢´Ë¶ñÁÇ∫Ëá∫ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©Ë∑ØÁ∑öÁöÑË±°Âæµ‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇÊ≠§Â§ñÔºå‰ªñÈùûÂ∏∏ÈóúÊ≥®Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÁ¶èÈü≥ÂèäÂÆ£ÊïôÔºåÂüπÈ§äÂ§ö‰ΩçÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÇ≥ÈÅìËÄÖ„ÄÇ

Kao Chun-ming (1929.6.6‚Äì2019.2.14) was a native of Tainan and a pastor of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan. He was the grandson of Kao Chang. Kao graduated from Tainan Theological College and Seminary and studied at Selly Oak College in Birmingham, UK. From 1957 to 1970, he served as the president of Yu-Shan Theological College and Seminary. Between 1970 and 1989, he was the General Secretary of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, during which the General Assembly issued three significant declarations: A Declaration and Recommendations on the National Affairs of Taiwan (1971), Our Appeal (1975), and The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Human Rights Declaration (1977).

During the discussions and after the publication of these declarations, the Kuomintang (KMT) government, which was still under authoritarian rule, closely monitored the church, conducting surveillance, recording meetings, and employing threats and inducements, even intervening in church agenda discussions. As the General Secretary and one of the drafters of these declarations, Kao Chun-ming faced immense pressure from the government. He was urged to resign from his position and leave Taiwan. Aware of the constant danger, he even prepared a farewell letter in case of unforeseen circumstances. After the third declaration, Kao was under government surveillance but was sheltered by missionaries Mr. and Mrs. Dan Whallon (Kao 2001: 262-263; Lin 2007: 249, 339).

In fact, because the government interpreted the third declaration as advocating ‚ÄúTaiwan independence,‚Äù the Security Bureau‚Äôs Investigation and Prevention Command Office established the ‚Äú819 Project‚Äù Command Center on August 19, convening relevant agencies to discuss countermeasures. The National Police Agency labeled Kao Chun-ming a ‚Äúconspirator,‚Äù advocating for his ‚Äúrepentance‚Äù through persuasion and attempting to remove him from office with the help of ‚Äúpatriotic figures‚Äù within the church. If these efforts failed, they suggested either ‚Äúexiling him abroad‚Äù or prosecuting him based on alleged illegal activities (Huang Kexian 2021: 30).

In 1979, the Kaohsiung Incident occurred, and the government launched a full-scale crackdown on those involved. One of the key figures, Shih Ming-teh, evaded capture for several days before being arrested. During his time in hiding, Kao Chun-ming and Elder Lin Wen-chen provided him with shelter. On April 24, 1980, Kao was arrested for harboring a fugitive and later sentenced to seven years in prison with five years of civil rights deprivation. However, he was released early in August 1984. During his imprisonment, Pope John Paul II sent the Vatican‚Äôs envoy to Taiwan, Monsignor Paul Giglio, to visit Kao, highlighting the international concern over the case (Anonymous 1982: 54-55; Kao 2001: 271-275).

In the 1970s, during Taiwan‚Äôs rapidly changing political landscape and ongoing authoritarian rule, Kao Chun-ming courageously opposed the government on issues of human rights, democracy, and national destiny. He remains a symbol of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan‚Äôs commitment to social justice. Additionally, he was deeply devoted to indigenous evangelism and missionary work, nurturing many indigenous preachers.

Wang Hsien-chih (1941.6.6‚Äì1996.3.29) was a native of Kaohsiung and an Anglican pastor. He was baptized in 1963 at the Evangelical Lutheran Truth Church. In 1968, he graduated from Tainan Theological College and Seminary, where he later taught from 1975 to 1993. In 1973, he obtained a Master of Theology degree from the General Theological Seminary of the Episcopal Church in New York. In 1978, he earned a Doctor of Theology degree from the South East Asia Graduate School of Theology, becoming its first Doctor of Theology, under the guidance of Professor Kosuke Koyama. His major works include The Concept of Nature in the Dao De Jing and Its Theological Significance and Collected Essays on Taiwanese Contextual Theology, establishing him as a key scholar in indigenous theology.

In 1977, when the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan issued The Human Rights Declaration of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, the declaration was officially adopted at the 25th General Assembly the following year. Wang was invited as a theological advisor to participate in the meeting, where he interpreted the declaration from the perspectives of democratic political principles and Christian theology, addressing the attending government officials.

Wang Hsien-chih was not only dedicated to the localization of theology but also actively participated in the democratic movement. In 1978, following the establishment of diplomatic relations between the United States and China, he was entrusted by senior leaders of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan to attend a U.S. congressional hearing on the Taiwan Relations Act in 1979, alongside Peng Ming-min and Huang Chang-hui.

At a 1979 theological seminar jointly organized by the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan and the Theological Committee of the Christian Conference of Asia, Wang officially introduced the term ‚ÄúContextual Theology of the Homeland‚Äù (Xiangtu Theology). This theology centers on the concept of xiangtu (‚Äúhomeland‚Äù), where xiang represents the people and their native place, and tu signifies the land and natural resources. He argued that ‚Äúhomeland‚Äù combined with ‚Äúsovereignty‚Äù constitutes a nation, forming the foundation of his theological framework for Taiwan‚Äôs national identity (Chuang Yat-tang 1997: 21-25).

‰∏≠ÂøÉÂ∞àÂÆ∂Âêà‰ΩúÔºåÂ∞áÁ•àÁ¶±ËàáÂøÉÁêÜËºîÂ∞éÁµêÂêàÔºåÁõ∏ËºîÁõ∏ÊàêÔºåÊìöÁ®±ÊàêÊïàÈ†óÁÇ∫ÁêÜÊÉ≥ÔºàÊûóÈùúÂÆú2018Ôºö116-122ÔºõËÄ∂Á©åÊúÉÈùíÂπ¥‰ΩøÂæíÂ∑•‰ΩúÂ∞èÁµÑ2004Ôºö127-129„ÄÅ284-286Ôºâ„ÄÇÁéãÊï¨ÂºòÁöÑË≤¢ÁçªÈô§‰∫ÜÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£Êé®ÂãïÁ•ûÊÅ©Âæ©ËààÈÅãÂãïÁöÑÈùàÊÄßÂ±§Èù¢ÔºåÂêåÊôÇ‰πüÊ∂µËìãÁµêÊßãÊÄßÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉË≠∞È°å‰∏¶Â∞ãÊ±ÇÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©ÔºåÂèØË¶ãÂÖ∂‰ø°‰ª∞ÈóúÊá∑ÁöÑÊï¥ÂÖ®ÊÄßÔºåÈ°õË¶Ü‰∫ÜÈùàÊÅ©ÈÅãÂãïÂíåÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©ÁÑ°Ê≥ïÂÖ±Â≠òÁöÑÊó¢ÂÆöÂç∞Ë±°„ÄÇ

Wang Richard Ching-hung (1934‚Äì1999.2.21) was born in Hankou and was a Catholic priest and a Jesuit brother. He graduated from the Civil Engineering Department at National Taiwan University and decided to enter the priesthood in 1958, joining the seminary. In 1962, he studied at the Baguio Theological Seminary in the Philippines. After his ordination in 1971, he became actively involved in the Catholic Charismatic Renewal Movement, becoming the first ethnic Chinese priest to participate in the movement since it was introduced to the Catholic Church in 1967.

However, during the 1970s, the Chinese Regional Bishops‚Äô Conference in Taiwan initially took a cautious stance toward the Charismatic Renewal Movement, which led to skepticism regarding Wang‚Äôs work. His practice of conducting ‚Äúdeliverance‚Äù prayers at the Kuangchi Cultural Center was particularly controversial and was even prohibited by Jesuit leadership. It was only after Zhang Chun-shen explained the movement to the bishops based on the theological perspective of Francis Sullivan, a professor at the Pontifical Gregorian University in Rome, that the movement gained acceptance within the church. Nevertheless, it was not until the 1990s that the Jesuits‚Äô negative perception of Wang and the movement began to change. During this period, Wang conducted research at Fu Jen Theological College and lived among Jesuits, fostering close interactions that helped ease concerns. His influence also inspired younger Jesuits, including Wu Ching-chi, Wu Po-jen, and Su A-yuan, who later became key leaders of the Charismatic Renewal Movement in Taiwan after Wang‚Äôs passing.

Although Wang was dedicated to promoting the Charismatic Renewal Movement, emphasizing ‚Äúbaptism in the Holy Spirit‚Äù and healing prayers, his faith did not dichotomize ‚Äúthis world‚Äù and ‚Äúthe next‚Äù or prioritize spirituality at the expense of social engagement. When he began practicing ‚Äúinner healing‚Äù prayers, he encountered individuals whose personal trauma was deeply intertwined with broader social issues, including provincial conflicts, ethnic tensions, intermarriage discrimination between mainlanders and Taiwanese, and labor exploitation. This led him to explore the structural social factors behind these personal struggles rather than viewing them solely through a spiritual lens.

In the late 1980s, Wang became increasingly involved in human rights advocacy, speaking out for the families of victims of the February 28 Incident. He collaborated with Christians from different denominations to organize commemorative worship services, exhibitions, and forums. Given the political sensitivity of the issue at the time, the bishops‚Äô conference explicitly prohibited priests and nuns from engaging in politically related activities, subjecting Wang to immense pressure from both the ruling party and the church hierarchy. Wang also critically observed that Taiwan‚Äôs Catholic Church was predominantly composed of mainlanders, making it inclined to sympathize with the Kuomintang government while lacking understanding of the historical suffering of native Taiwanese. He argued that true inner healing must begin with a deep acknowledgment of the pain endured by native Taiwanese.

Because some of his relatives had ties to the Kuomintang regime, Wang was well aware of its systemic problems. As director of the Kuangchi Cultural Center, he took an outspoken stance against the Kuomintang, which led to backlash from certain church members. Additionally, his inner healing ministry was seen as filling a gap in Taiwan‚Äôs mental health resources before the 1980s. Since 1978, he had collaborated with experts from the Hua-Ming Psychological Counseling Center, integrating prayer with psychological counseling, reportedly yielding significant results (Lin Jing-yi 2018: 116-122; Jesuit Youth Apostolate Working Group 2004: 127-129, 284-286).

Wang Richard Ching-hung‚Äôs contributions extended beyond the spiritual aspects of the Charismatic Renewal Movement. He also addressed structural social issues and sought social justice, demonstrating the holistic nature of his faith. His work challenged the conventional notion that the Charismatic Movement and social justice were incompatible, proving that spirituality and activism could coexist.

Lin Chi-Ping (1938.5.22‚Äì2024.4.27) was born in Changsha, Hunan, and moved to Taiwan in 1949. He was a scholar of Chinese Christian history, a co-founder of Cosmic Light magazine, the founder of the Graduate Institute of Religious Studies at Chung Yuan Christian University, and a recipient of the 12th Ten Outstanding Young Persons Award in the Republic of China.

Lin graduated from the Graduate Institute of Diplomacy at National Chengchi University in 1966, with a thesis titled A Study of the Ryukyu Incident. In 1973, senior Christian writer Liu Yi-ling wrote to Lin, urging him to establish Cosmic Light magazine as a successor to Lighthouse, a Christian publication that had ceased in 1970. Lin took on the task, adopting a ‚Äúpre-evangelistic‚Äù cultural missionary approach to the magazine, which was innovative within the conservative Chinese Christian community at the time. However, this direction diverged from Liu Yi-ling‚Äôs original intent of directly promoting Christian beliefs and faced skepticism from church leaders, including Wu Yong. Lin argued that overt Christian symbolism and direct criticism of Chinese culture were not beneficial to missionary efforts (Lin Chi-Ping 2023: 114-126).

Beyond founding the pre-evangelistic magazine, Lin Chi-Ping led the rise of Chinese Christian historical studies in the 1970s. He published two seminal works, Essays on Christianity and China‚Äôs Modernization (1970) and Christianity and China (1975). His primary academic concern at the time was addressing anti-Christian discourse by engaging with themes of indigenization and modernization, leading him to shift from studying Sino-British diplomatic history to Chinese church history. In Essays on Christianity and China‚Äôs Modernization, Lin examined figures such as Robert Morrison, William A.P. Martin, and Sun Yat-sen, highlighting Christianity‚Äôs role in China‚Äôs modernization and using this perspective to defend the faith. He argued that Christianity had not been fairly treated in Chinese-language academia, particularly in acknowledging its contributions. He also suggested that the historical conflict between Christianity and Chinese culture might stem from pre-existing deep cultural elements, thus emphasizing the importance of indigenization (Tao Feiya & Yang Weihua 2009: 169; Tseng Ching-pao 2011: 19-25).

Additionally, Lin established a literary and historical fellowship at his residence, encouraging Christian students to pursue studies in literature and history. He also invited university students from these fields to read and translate Western books on Chinese Christian history and missionary memoirs. Scholars who later became leading figures in the field, such as Li Jin-qiang, Zha Shi-jie, Wei Wai-yang, and Chen Yi-ping, were among those who participated (Lin Chi-Ping 2023: 204).

Lin played a crucial catalytic role in the study of Chinese church history in China, Hong Kong, and Taiwan. In 2007, he was awarded the ‚ÄúAcademic Achievement Award‚Äù by the Chinese Church History research community in Hong Kong in recognition of his contributions and accomplishments in the field.

                ËëõÁêÜÁø∞ÔºàBilly Franklin Graham, 1918.11.7-2018.2.21Ôºâ„ÄÇÁîüÊñºÁæéÂúãÂåóÂç°ÁæÖ‰æÜÁ¥çÂ∑ûÂ§èÊ¥õÁâπÔºåÁÇ∫ËëóÂêçÁ¶èÈü≥Ê¥æ‰ΩàÈÅìÂÆ∂„ÄÇ1936Âπ¥ÈÄ≤ÂÖ•Âü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æÁöÑÈÆë‰ºØÁìäÊñØÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºàBob Jones UniversityÔºâÔºåÂæåÂõ†‰∏çÈÅ©ÊáâÂö¥Ê†ºÁöÑÂü∫Ë¶Å‰∏ªÁæ©Ê∞õÂúçËΩâÂæÄ‰ΩõÁæÖÈáåÈÅîËÅñÁ∂ìÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºàFlorida Bible InstituteÔºâÔºåÂèàÂú®1940Âπ¥Â∞±ËÆÄÊÉ†È†ìÂ≠∏Èô¢ÔºàWheaton CollegeÔºâ„ÄÇ‰ªñÂéüÂÖàÊòØÈï∑ËÄÅÊúÉËÉåÊôØÔºå‰ΩÜÂú®‰ΩõÁæÖÈáåÈÅîËÅñÁ∂ìÂ≠∏Èô¢Ê±ÇÂ≠∏ÊúüÈñìËΩâÂêëÊµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÔºå1939Âπ¥Êé•ÂèóËÅñÁ¥ÑÁø∞Êµ∏‰ø°ÊúÉÂçîÊúÉÊåâÁ´ãÊàêÁÇ∫‰ΩàÈÅìÂÆ∂„ÄÇ1950Âπ¥‰ªñÊàêÁ´ãËëõÁêÜÁø∞‰ΩàÈÅìÂúòÔºå‰∏¶ÈñãÂßãÂæÄ‰∏ñÁïåÂêÑÂú∞ËàâË°å‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÊàêÁÇ∫ÂúãÈöõÁ¶èÈü≥Ê¥æÁü•Âêç‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇ1950Âπ¥‰ª£ÔºåÂú®ËëõÁêÜÁø∞Êñº‰øùÂÆàÊ¥æÈô£Ááü‰∏≠Â∞çÊñºÊïôÁæ©Ëß£ÈáãÂíåÂ∞çÂ§©‰∏ªÊïô„ÄÅ‰∫îÊó¨ÁØÄÊ¥æÁ≠âÂü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æÊéíÊñ•ÁöÑÂúòÈ´îÊâÄÂ±ïÁèæÁõ∏Â∞çÈñãÊòé„ÄÅÂåÖÂÆπÁöÑÊÖãÂ∫¶ÂΩ±Èüø‰∏ãÔºåËàáÂü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æÂàÜÈÅìÊèöÈë£ÁöÑ„ÄåÊñ∞Á¶èÈü≥Ê¥æ„ÄçÈÄêÊº∏ÂΩ¢ÊàêÔºåÈõñÂêåÁÇ∫‰øùÂÆà‰ø°‰ª∞Èô£ÁáüÔºå‰ΩÜÁõ∏Â∞çÈñãÊîæ‰∏îÊäµÊäóÂèçÊô∫‰∏ªÁæ©„ÄÇ‰ªñÂú®1956Âπ¥ÂâµÁ´ã„Ää‰ªäÊó•Âü∫Áù£ÊïôÈõúË™å„ÄãÔºåÁÇ∫‰ª£Ë°®Êñ∞Á¶èÈü≥Ê¥æÁ´ãÂ†¥ÁöÑÂàäÁâ©ÔºàÈ¶¨‰∏Å2020Ôºö259-260ÔºõËë£Ê±üÈôΩ2004Ôºö32Ôºâ„ÄÇËëõÁêÜÁø∞ÊåÅÈÆÆÊòéÂèçÂÖ±Á´ãÂ†¥Ôºå1970Âπ¥‰ª£ÈñãÂßãÊ∂âÂÖ•Ëá∫ÁÅ£ÁöÑÊîøÊ≤ªÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§„ÄÇ1971Âπ¥‰∏≠ËèØÊ∞ëÂúãÈÄÄÂá∫ËÅØÂêàÂúãÊôÇÔºå‰ªñÊõæÊâÆÊºîÁ©øÊ¢≠ÊñºËá∫ÁÅ£ÂíåËèØÂ∫ú‰πãÈñìÁöÑÂ±Ö‰∏≠ËßíËâ≤Ôºå‰∏¶ËàáÂ≠£ËæõÂêâ„ÄÅÂ∞ºÂÖãÊ£ÆÁ≠â‰∫∫Êé•Ëß∏„ÄÇ1975Âπ¥Ëî£‰ªãÁü≥ÈÅé‰∏ñÔºåËëõÁêÜÁø∞Âú®ËèØÂ∫úÁÇ∫ÂÖ∂‰∏ªÊåÅËøΩÊÄùÁ¶ÆÊãú„ÄÇÂêåÂπ¥10ÊúàÂ∫ïËá≥11ÊúàÂàù‰æÜÂè∞ËàâËæ¶Â§ßÂûã‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉÔºåÊ≠§‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉËàâË°åÊôÇÈñìÂéüÊì¨ÁâπÂà•ÈÖçÂêàËî£‰ªãÁü≥ÁîüÊó•ÔºåÂú®‰ΩàÈÅìÂêåÊôÇÁÇ∫ÂÖ∂Á•ùÂ£ΩÔºå‰∏¶ÁµÑÁ•ùÂ£ΩËá¥Êï¨ÂúòÂâçÂæÄÁ∏ΩÁµ±Â∫úÔºåÊú™ÊñôËî£‰ªãÁü≥Âú®4ÊúàÈÄù‰∏ñ„ÄÇÊõæÊÖ∂Ë±πË™çÁÇ∫Ëá∫ÁÅ£ËàáÂúãÊ∞ëÈª®ÂíåËî£‰ªãÁü≥Ë¶™ËøëÁöÑ„ÄåÈª®ÂúãÂü∫Áù£Âæí„ÄçÂú®ÂÇ≥ÊïôÂíåÂ§ñ‰∫§ÈõôÊñπÈù¢Âà©Áî®ËëõÁêÜÁø∞ÁöÑÂΩ±ÈüøÂäõÔºåÂ∞áÂÖ∂Âú®Âè∞‰ΩàÈÅìÊúÉËàáËî£‰ªãÁü≥ÁöÑÁ•ùÂ£ΩÁµêÂêà„ÄÇÁÑ∂ËÄåÔºåÁî±ÊñºËëõÁêÜÁø∞ËàáÂü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æ‰øùÊåÅË∑ùÈõ¢Ôºå‰ª•Âü∫Ë¶ÅÊ¥æÁÇ∫Á•ûÂ≠∏Âü∫Ë™øÁöÑ„ÄåÈª®ÂúãÂü∫Áù£Âæí„Äç‰πüÂõ†Ê≠§ËàáÂÖ∂Êº∏Ë°åÊº∏ÈÅ†ÔºàÊõæÊÖ∂Ë±π2016Ôºö236-239Ôºâ„ÄÇ

                Billy Franklin Graham (1918.11.7‚Äì2018.2.21)

                Born in Charlotte, North Carolina, USA, Billy Graham was a renowned evangelical preacher. In 1936, he enrolled at Bob Jones University, a fundamentalist institution, but later transferred to Florida Bible Institute due to difficulties adapting to the strict fundamentalist environment. In 1940, he attended Wheaton College. Initially from a Presbyterian background, he shifted toward the Baptist tradition during his studies at Florida Bible Institute and was ordained as an evangelist in 1939 by the St. John‚Äôs Baptist Association.

                In 1950, Graham founded the Billy Graham Evangelistic Association and began conducting evangelistic crusades worldwide, becoming an internationally recognized figure in evangelical circles. During the 1950s, his relatively open and inclusive attitude towards doctrinal interpretation and toward groups often rejected by fundamentalists, such as Catholics and Pentecostals, contributed to the emergence of the ‚ÄúNew Evangelical‚Äù movement. Although still within the conservative faith tradition, the New Evangelicals were comparatively more open and resisted anti-intellectualism. In 1956, Graham established Christianity Today, a publication representing the New Evangelical perspective (Martin 2020: 259-260; Dong Jiangyang 2004: 32).

                Graham held a staunch anti-communist stance and became involved in Taiwan‚Äôs political and diplomatic affairs in the 1970s. In 1971, when the Republic of China withdrew from the United Nations, he played a mediatory role between Taiwan and Washington, engaging with figures such as Henry Kissinger and Richard Nixon. After the death of Chiang Kai-shek in 1975, Graham presided over a memorial service for him in Washington, D.C. Later that year, from late October to early November, he visited Taiwan to hold large-scale evangelistic meetings. These events were originally planned to coincide with Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs birthday, incorporating a celebration and tribute delegation to the Presidential Office. However, Chiang passed away in April.

                Researcher Zeng Qingbao (2016: 236-239) argues that Taiwan‚Äôs pro-Kuomintang (KMT) ‚Äúparty-state Christians‚Äù leveraged Graham‚Äôs influence for both missionary and diplomatic purposes, linking his evangelistic crusades with Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs birthday celebrations. However, since Graham distanced himself from fundamentalism, theologically fundamentalist ‚Äúparty-state Christians‚Äù gradually grew apart from him.

1982Ôºö54-55ÔºõÈ´ò‰øäÊòé2001Ôºö271-275Ôºâ„ÄÇÈ´ò‰øäÊòéÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£Â±ÄÂã¢È©üËÆä‰∏î‰ªçÊòØÂ®ÅÊ¨äÊôÇÊúüÁöÑ1970Âπ¥‰ª£ÔºåÊï¢ÊñºÁÇ∫‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄÅÊ∞ë‰∏ª„ÄÅÂúãÂÆ∂ÂâçÈÄîÁ≠âË≠∞È°åËÄåËàáÂ®ÅÊ¨äÊîøÂ∫úÂ∞çÊäóÔºåËá≥‰ªä‰ªçË¢´Ë¶ñÁÇ∫Ëá∫ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©Ë∑ØÁ∑öÁöÑË±°Âæµ‰∫∫Áâ©„ÄÇÊ≠§Â§ñÔºå‰ªñÈùûÂ∏∏ÈóúÊ≥®Âéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÁ¶èÈü≥ÂèäÂÆ£ÊïôÔºåÂüπÈ§äÂ§ö‰ΩçÂéü‰ΩèÊ∞ëÂÇ≥ÈÅìËÄÖ„ÄÇ

                Kao Chun-ming (1929.6.6‚Äì2019.2.14) was a native of Tainan and a pastor of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan. He was the grandson of Kao Chang. Kao graduated from Tainan Theological College and Seminary and studied at Selly Oak College in Birmingham, UK. From 1957 to 1970, he served as the president of Yu-Shan Theological College and Seminary. Between 1970 and 1989, he was the General Secretary of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, during which the General Assembly issued three significant declarations: A Declaration and Recommendations on the National Affairs of Taiwan (1971), Our Appeal (1975), and The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Human Rights Declaration (1977).

                During the discussions and after the publication of these declarations, the Kuomintang (KMT) government, which was still under authoritarian rule, closely monitored the church, conducting surveillance, recording meetings, and employing threats and inducements, even intervening in church agenda discussions. As the General Secretary and one of the drafters of these declarations, Kao Chun-ming faced immense pressure from the government. He was urged to resign from his position and leave Taiwan. Aware of the constant danger, he even prepared a farewell letter in case of unforeseen circumstances. After the third declaration, Kao was under government surveillance but was sheltered by missionaries Mr. and Mrs. Dan Whallon (Kao 2001: 262-263; Lin 2007: 249, 339).

                In fact, because the government interpreted the third declaration as advocating ‚ÄúTaiwan independence,‚Äù the Security Bureau‚Äôs Investigation and Prevention Command Office established the ‚Äú819 Project‚Äù Command Center on August 19, convening relevant agencies to discuss countermeasures. The National Police Agency labeled Kao Chun-ming a ‚Äúconspirator,‚Äù advocating for his ‚Äúrepentance‚Äù through persuasion and attempting to remove him from office with the help of ‚Äúpatriotic figures‚Äù within the church. If these efforts failed, they suggested either ‚Äúexiling him abroad‚Äù or prosecuting him based on alleged illegal activities (Huang Kexian 2021: 30).

                In 1979, the Kaohsiung Incident occurred, and the government launched a full-scale crackdown on those involved. One of the key figures, Shih Ming-teh, evaded capture for several days before being arrested. During his time in hiding, Kao Chun-ming and Elder Lin Wen-chen provided him with shelter. On April 24, 1980, Kao was arrested for harboring a fugitive and later sentenced to seven years in prison with five years of civil rights deprivation. However, he was released early in August 1984. During his imprisonment, Pope John Paul II sent the Vatican‚Äôs envoy to Taiwan, Monsignor Paul Giglio, to visit Kao, highlighting the international concern over the case (Anonymous 1982: 54-55; Kao 2001: 271-275).

                In the 1970s, during Taiwan‚Äôs rapidly changing political landscape and ongoing authoritarian rule, Kao Chun-ming courageously opposed the government on issues of human rights, democracy, and national destiny. He remains a symbol of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan‚Äôs commitment to social justice. Additionally, he was deeply devoted to indigenous evangelism and missionary work, nurturing many indigenous preachers.

                Wang Hsien-chih (1941.6.6‚Äì1996.3.29) was a native of Kaohsiung and an Anglican pastor. He was baptized in 1963 at the Evangelical Lutheran Truth Church. In 1968, he graduated from Tainan Theological College and Seminary, where he later taught from 1975 to 1993. In 1973, he obtained a Master of Theology degree from the General Theological Seminary of the Episcopal Church in New York. In 1978, he earned a Doctor of Theology degree from the South East Asia Graduate School of Theology, becoming its first Doctor of Theology, under the guidance of Professor Kosuke Koyama. His major works include The Concept of Nature in the Dao De Jing and Its Theological Significance and Collected Essays on Taiwanese Contextual Theology, establishing him as a key scholar in indigenous theology.

                In 1977, when the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan issued The Human Rights Declaration of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan, the declaration was officially adopted at the 25th General Assembly the following year. Wang was invited as a theological advisor to participate in the meeting, where he interpreted the declaration from the perspectives of democratic political principles and Christian theology, addressing the attending government officials.

                Wang Hsien-chih was not only dedicated to the localization of theology but also actively participated in the democratic movement. In 1978, following the establishment of diplomatic relations between the United States and China, he was entrusted by senior leaders of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan to attend a U.S. congressional hearing on the Taiwan Relations Act in 1979, alongside Peng Ming-min and Huang Chang-hui.

                At a 1979 theological seminar jointly organized by the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan and the Theological Committee of the Christian Conference of Asia, Wang officially introduced the term ‚ÄúContextual Theology of the Homeland‚Äù (Xiangtu Theology). This theology centers on the concept of xiangtu (‚Äúhomeland‚Äù), where xiang represents the people and their native place, and tu signifies the land and natural resources. He argued that ‚Äúhomeland‚Äù combined with ‚Äúsovereignty‚Äù constitutes a nation, forming the foundation of his theological framework for Taiwan‚Äôs national identity (Chuang Yat-tang 1997: 21-25).

‰∏≠ÂøÉÂ∞àÂÆ∂Âêà‰ΩúÔºåÂ∞áÁ•àÁ¶±ËàáÂøÉÁêÜËºîÂ∞éÁµêÂêàÔºåÁõ∏ËºîÁõ∏ÊàêÔºåÊìöÁ®±ÊàêÊïàÈ†óÁÇ∫ÁêÜÊÉ≥ÔºàÊûóÈùúÂÆú2018Ôºö116-122ÔºõËÄ∂Á©åÊúÉÈùíÂπ¥‰ΩøÂæíÂ∑•‰ΩúÂ∞èÁµÑ2004Ôºö127-129„ÄÅ284-286Ôºâ„ÄÇÁéãÊï¨ÂºòÁöÑË≤¢ÁçªÈô§‰∫ÜÂú®Ëá∫ÁÅ£Êé®ÂãïÁ•ûÊÅ©Âæ©ËààÈÅãÂãïÁöÑÈùàÊÄßÂ±§Èù¢ÔºåÂêåÊôÇ‰πüÊ∂µËìãÁµêÊßãÊÄßÁöÑÁ§æÊúÉË≠∞È°å‰∏¶Â∞ãÊ±ÇÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©ÔºåÂèØË¶ãÂÖ∂‰ø°‰ª∞ÈóúÊá∑ÁöÑÊï¥ÂÖ®ÊÄßÔºåÈ°õË¶Ü‰∫ÜÈùàÊÅ©ÈÅãÂãïÂíåÁ§æÊúÉÂÖ¨Áæ©ÁÑ°Ê≥ïÂÖ±Â≠òÁöÑÊó¢ÂÆöÂç∞Ë±°„ÄÇ

                Wang Richard Ching-hung (1934‚Äì1999.2.21) was born in Hankou and was a Catholic priest and a Jesuit brother. He graduated from the Civil Engineering Department at National Taiwan University and decided to enter the priesthood in 1958, joining the seminary. In 1962, he studied at the Baguio Theological Seminary in the Philippines. After his ordination in 1971, he became actively involved in the Catholic Charismatic Renewal Movement, becoming the first ethnic Chinese priest to participate in the movement since it was introduced to the Catholic Church in 1967.

                However, during the 1970s, the Chinese Regional Bishops‚Äô Conference in Taiwan initially took a cautious stance toward the Charismatic Renewal Movement, which led to skepticism regarding Wang‚Äôs work. His practice of conducting ‚Äúdeliverance‚Äù prayers at the Kuangchi Cultural Center was particularly controversial and was even prohibited by Jesuit leadership. It was only after Zhang Chun-shen explained the movement to the bishops based on the theological perspective of Francis Sullivan, a professor at the Pontifical Gregorian University in Rome, that the movement gained acceptance within the church. Nevertheless, it was not until the 1990s that the Jesuits‚Äô negative perception of Wang and the movement began to change. During this period, Wang conducted research at Fu Jen Theological College and lived among Jesuits, fostering close interactions that helped ease concerns. His influence also inspired younger Jesuits, including Wu Ching-chi, Wu Po-jen, and Su A-yuan, who later became key leaders of the Charismatic Renewal Movement in Taiwan after Wang‚Äôs passing.

                Although Wang was dedicated to promoting the Charismatic Renewal Movement, emphasizing ‚Äúbaptism in the Holy Spirit‚Äù and healing prayers, his faith did not dichotomize ‚Äúthis world‚Äù and ‚Äúthe next‚Äù or prioritize spirituality at the expense of social engagement. When he began practicing ‚Äúinner healing‚Äù prayers, he encountered individuals whose personal trauma was deeply intertwined with broader social issues, including provincial conflicts, ethnic tensions, intermarriage discrimination between mainlanders and Taiwanese, and labor exploitation. This led him to explore the structural social factors behind these personal struggles rather than viewing them solely through a spiritual lens.

                In the late 1980s, Wang became increasingly involved in human rights advocacy, speaking out for the families of victims of the February 28 Incident. He collaborated with Christians from different denominations to organize commemorative worship services, exhibitions, and forums. Given the political sensitivity of the issue at the time, the bishops‚Äô conference explicitly prohibited priests and nuns from engaging in politically related activities, subjecting Wang to immense pressure from both the ruling party and the church hierarchy. Wang also critically observed that Taiwan‚Äôs Catholic Church was predominantly composed of mainlanders, making it inclined to sympathize with the Kuomintang government while lacking understanding of the historical suffering of native Taiwanese. He argued that true inner healing must begin with a deep acknowledgment of the pain endured by native Taiwanese.

                Because some of his relatives had ties to the Kuomintang regime, Wang was well aware of its systemic problems. As director of the Kuangchi Cultural Center, he took an outspoken stance against the Kuomintang, which led to backlash from certain church members. Additionally, his inner healing ministry was seen as filling a gap in Taiwan‚Äôs mental health resources before the 1980s. Since 1978, he had collaborated with experts from the Hua-Ming Psychological Counseling Center, integrating prayer with psychological counseling, reportedly yielding significant results (Lin Jing-yi 2018: 116-122; Jesuit Youth Apostolate Working Group 2004: 127-129, 284-286).

                Wang Richard Ching-hung‚Äôs contributions extended beyond the spiritual aspects of the Charismatic Renewal Movement. He also addressed structural social issues and sought social justice, demonstrating the holistic nature of his faith. His work challenged the conventional notion that the Charismatic Movement and social justice were incompatible, proving that spirituality and activism could coexist.

Âπ¥Âíå                 Lin Chi-Ping (1938.5.22‚Äì2024.4.27) was born in Changsha, Hunan, and moved to Taiwan in 1949. He was a scholar of Chinese Christian history, a co-founder of Cosmic Light magazine, the founder of the Graduate Institute of Religious Studies at Chung Yuan Christian University, and a recipient of the 12th Ten Outstanding Young Persons Award in the Republic of China.

                Lin graduated from the Graduate Institute of Diplomacy at National Chengchi University in 1966, with a thesis titled A Study of the Ryukyu Incident. In 1973, senior Christian writer Liu Yi-ling wrote to Lin, urging him to establish Cosmic Light magazine as a successor to Lighthouse, a Christian publication that had ceased in 1970. Lin took on the task, adopting a ‚Äúpre-evangelistic‚Äù cultural missionary approach to the magazine, which was innovative within the conservative Chinese Christian community at the time. However, this direction diverged from Liu Yi-ling‚Äôs original intent of directly promoting Christian beliefs and faced skepticism from church leaders, including Wu Yong. Lin argued that overt Christian symbolism and direct criticism of Chinese culture were not beneficial to missionary efforts (Lin Chi-Ping 2023: 114-126).

                Beyond founding the pre-evangelistic magazine, Lin Chi-Ping led the rise of Chinese Christian historical studies in the 1970s. He published two seminal works, Essays on Christianity and China‚Äôs Modernization

  1. and Christianity and China (1975). His primary academic concern at the time was addressing anti-Christian discourse by engaging with themes of indigenization and modernization, leading him to shift from studying Sino-British diplomatic history to Chinese church history. In Essays on Christianity and China‚Äôs Modernization, Lin examined figures such as Robert Morrison, William A.P. Martin, and Sun Yat-sen, highlighting Christianity‚Äôs role in China‚Äôs modernization and using this perspective to defend the faith. He argued that Christianity had not been fairly treated in Chinese-language academia, particularly in acknowledging its contributions. He also suggested that the historical conflict between Christianity and Chinese culture might stem from pre-existing deep cultural elements, thus emphasizing the importance of indigenization (Tao Feiya & Yang Weihua 2009: 169; Tseng Ching-pao 2011: 19-25).

    Additionally, Lin established a literary and historical fellowship at his residence, encouraging Christian students to pursue studies in literature and history. He also invited university students from these fields to read and translate Western books on Chinese Christian history and missionary memoirs. Scholars who later became leading figures in the field, such as Li Jin-qiang, Zha Shi-jie, Wei Wai-yang, and Chen Yi-ping, were among those who participated (Lin Chi-Ping 2023: 204).

    Lin played a crucial catalytic role in the study of Chinese church history in China, Hong Kong, and Taiwan. In 2007, he was awarded the ‚ÄúAcademic Achievement Award‚Äù by the Chinese Church History research community in Hong Kong in recognition of his contributions and accomplishments in the field.

    China Evangelical Seminary (CES) traces its origins to a 1965 summer conference held by the Taiwan Missionary Fellowship at Sun Moon Lake, where Clyde W. Taylor, then General Secretary of the World Evangelical Fellowship, proposed the establishment of a graduate-level theological seminary in Taiwan. The seminary was officially founded in 1970, launching a three-year Master of Divinity program with an initial cohort of seven students. The first president was James Taylor III, the academic dean was Chao Tien-en, and the chairman of the board was Wu Yong. In 1975, CES established Taiwan‚Äôs first department of missiology.

    In its early years, the seminary borrowed facilities from the Bible Baptist Church on Fulin Road in Shilin and the Bread of Life Christian Church on Xingfu Street. In 1976, the board decided to secure a permanent campus and began fundraising in late January. They quickly secured a $12,000 funding commitment, and construction commenced on Christmas Day of that year. The new campus building on Tingzhou Road was completed and inaugurated in 1979, serving as the seminary‚Äôs base until its demolition and reconstruction in 2024 (Wang Chun-yi 2024; Li Mu-quan 2020: 10-13; Rubinstein 1991: 96-99).

    CES was Taiwan‚Äôs first seminary exclusively admitting university graduates. This policy stemmed from concerns that theological seminaries had become an alternative for those who had failed to enter higher education institutions, using seminary training as a means to secure a future in ministry. Some missionaries in Taiwan disapproved of this trend, fearing that theological education would be compromised (Rubinstein 1991: 96).

    Beyond training young intellectuals as ministers, the seminary‚Äôs founding was also influenced by the evangelical movement within Taiwan‚Äôs Mandarin-speaking churches, which sought to counter liberal theology and the ecumenical movement. CES positioned itself as an interdenominational (or, as some scholars argue, non-denominational) institution, distinguishing itself from foreign mission-founded denominational seminaries while emphasizing localization. Additionally, it sought to differentiate itself from ‚Äúliberal‚Äù seminaries affiliated with the Presbyterian Church and the World Council of Churches. From its inception, CES upheld a conservative evangelical stance, particularly emphasizing biblical inerrancy as a response to liberal theology. It provided an alternative for conservative Christians pursuing theological education.

    In 1973, Chao Tien-en convened a meeting with Teng Chin-hui, Wang Yong-xin, James Taylor III, and Lin Dao-liang to explore the possibility of merging CES with the China Graduate School of Theology in Hong Kong. However, after assessing the distinct theological education needs of the two regions, Wu Yong and others decided to abandon the plan (Tseng Ching-pao 2016: 245-257, 278-283; Lin Shu-ching 2004: 15).

Â∞àÊ°à„ÄçÊåáÊèÆÈÉ®ÔºåÂä†Âº∑Áõ£ÊéßÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉËàáÈ´ò‰øäÊòé„ÄÇÁÑ∂ËÄåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉÊñº1978Âπ¥3Êúà31Êó•ÂÖ¨ÈñãÊæÑÊ∏ÖÔºåÊâπË©ïÂâçËø∞Â†±Â∞éÂíåÁêÜËß£„Äå‰∏çÂØüÊòé‰∫ãÂØ¶Ôºå‰∏ç‰∫ÜËß£ÁôºË°®‰∫∫Ê¨äÂÆ£Ë®Ä‰πãËÉåÊôØËàáÂãïÊ©üÔºå‰Ωú‰∏çÁõ°Ë≤¨‰ªªÁöÑÂ†±Â∞éÔºåÂö¥ÈáçÂç±ÂÆ≥ÊïôÊúÉÂêçË≠ΩÂíåÂèØËÉΩÈÄ†ÊàêÊïôÊúÉÂàÜË£Ç„ÄçÔºå‰∏¶ÊåáÂá∫Ë©≤ÂÆ£Ë®ÄÁöÑËÉåÊôØÂíåÂãïÊ©üÂú®Êñº„ÄåÊèêÈÜíÁæéÂúãÁ∏ΩÁµ±Âç°ÁâπÂàáÂãøÂá∫Ë≥£Âè∞ÁÅ£‰∏ÄÂçÉ‰∏ÉÁôæËê¨ÁôæÂßìÁöÑ‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄçÔºåÂëºÁ±≤ÁæéÂúãÂãøÂõ†ÊÄ•Ê¨≤Ëàá‰∏≠ÂÖ±Èóú‰øÇ„ÄåÊ≠£Â∏∏Âåñ„ÄçÔºåËÄåÂøΩË¶ñËá∫ÁÅ£ÁöÑÁîüÂ≠ò„ÄÇË©≤ÊúÉ‰∏¶ÊæÑÊ∏ÖÔºå„ÄåÊñ∞ËÄåÁç®Á´ãÁöÑÂúãÂÆ∂„ÄçÁÑ°ÈóúÂè∞Áç®ÔºåËÄåÊòØ‰ª•‰∏≠ËèØÊ∞ëÂúãÂúãÊ∞ëË∫´‰ªΩÔºå„Äå‰øÉË´ãÊîøÂ∫úÊé°ÂèñÊúâÊïàÊé™ÊñΩÔºå‰æÜÂª∫Ë®≠ÁêÜÊÉ≥ÁöÑÂúãÂÆ∂„ÄçÔºàÁÑ°‰ΩúËÄÖ1977ÔºõÁÑ°‰ΩúËÄÖ1978ÔºõÊõæÊÖ∂Ë±π2015Ôºö312-316ÔºõÁéãÊÜ≤Ê≤ª1988Ôºö414-418ÔºõÊûóÈáëÊ∞¥347-348Ôºö2003ÔºõÈÑ≠Áù¶Áæ§2017Ôºö68-72Ôºâ„ÄÇÁÑ∂ËÄåÔºåÂæå‰æÜÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÈ†òË¢ñÂíåÂ≠∏ËÄÖÁöÑË©ÆÈáãÂíåËß£ËÆÄË°®Á§∫ÔºåÊ≠§‰∏ÄÂÆ£Ë®ÄÂèØË¨ÇÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂè∞Áç®Á´ãÂ†¥ÁöÑÈñãÁ´ØÔºåÁï∂ÊôÇÁöÑ„ÄåÊæÑÊ∏Ö„ÄçÁÇ∫ÊôÇ‰ª£Â£ìÂäõ‰∏ã‰∏çÂæó‰∏çÁÑ∂ÁöÑÁµêÊûúÔºàÊûóËä≥‰ª≤2007Ôºö116Ôºâ„ÄÇÊ≠§Â§ñÔºå1979Âπ¥ÁæéÈ∫óÂ≥∂‰∫ã‰ª∂ÁàÜÁôºÔºåÊúâ‰∏çÂ∞ëÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁâßÈï∑„ÄÅÊúÉÂèãËàáÁ•ûÂ≠∏ÁîüÂèÉËàáÂÖ∂‰∏≠ÔºåÈ´ò‰øäÊòéÂõ†ËóèÂåøÊñΩÊòéÂæ∑ËÄåÈÅ≠Âà§ÂàëÔºåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉËá™Ê≠§‰πüÈñãÂßãËàáÈª®Â§ñÈÅãÂãïÂêàÊµÅ„ÄÇ

    The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) issued three significant declarations during the 1970s in response to political turbulence and crises, including Taiwan‚Äôs withdrawal from the United Nations, the severance of diplomatic ties between Taiwan and the U.S., the establishment of U.S.-China diplomatic relations, and the death of Chiang Kai-shek.

    In 1971, the PCT issued A Declaration and Recommendations on the National Affairs of Taiwan. The initial draft was co-authored by Baptist pastor Chou Lien-hua, Methodist missionary T. Otto Nall, and Presbyterian missionary Daniel Beeby. Later, Lutheran Bishop Chang Chi-tang joined the drafting process, with Kao Chun-ming serving as convener. The declaration was originally intended to be released under the ‚ÄúChurch Cooperation Committee.‚Äù However, other Christian groups, including the Catholic Church, Methodist Church, Lutheran Church, and Anglican Church, refused to sign it. Kao Chun-ming then brought the document back to the Presbyterian Church for discussion and revision. On December 29, 1971, the PCT officially released the declaration, calling on the international community to recognize Taiwan‚Äôs human rights and right to self-determination while warning of the risk of Taiwan being annexed by the communist regime. Domestically, it advocated for the full re-election of national representatives. Due to the modifications made by the PCT, Chou Lien-hua withdrew from the Church Cooperation Committee in protest (Wu Chang-hsing 2008: 284; Kao Chun-ming 2001: 229-230).

    Anticipating the government‚Äôs reaction, the PCT leveraged its transnational religious network and sent the declaration to Pope Paul VI, the U.S. State Department, the Presbyterian Church in Canada, and the National Christian Council of Japan, seeking international church support to enhance the legitimacy of the statement (Huang Kexian 2021: 27).

    In 1975, the PCT issued Our Appeal following the confiscation of Romanized-script Bibles that year. The declaration urged the government to respect religious freedom and emphasized the importance of maintaining a positive relationship between the church and the state.

    In 1977, the PCT released The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan Human Rights Declaration, asserting that ‚Äúthe future of Taiwan should be decided by its 17 million residents‚Äù and introducing the idea of making Taiwan ‚Äúa new and independent nation.‚Äù This statement led to widespread criticism in the media, with accusations that the PCT was advocating Taiwan independence and endangering national security. The government viewed the declaration as a clear pro-independence stance, prompting the Security Bureau‚Äôs Investigation and Prevention Command Office to establish the ‚Äú819 Project Command Center‚Äù on August 19, 1977, to intensify surveillance of the PCT and Kao Chun-ming.

    On March 31, 1978, the PCT General Assembly issued a public clarification, criticizing media reports and interpretations of the declaration as irresponsible, misleading, and harmful to the church‚Äôs reputation. The PCT explained that the declaration aimed to remind U.S. President Jimmy Carter not to betray the human rights of Taiwan‚Äôs 17 million people in the process of normalizing U.S.-China relations. The church urged the U.S. not to overlook Taiwan‚Äôs survival for the sake of diplomatic normalization with Beijing. The PCT also clarified that the phrase ‚Äúa new and independent nation‚Äù did not advocate for Taiwan independence but rather encouraged the government to take effective measures to build an ideal nation within the framework of the Republic of China (Anonymous 1977; Anonymous 1978; Tseng Ching-pao 2015: 312-316; Wang Hsien-chih 1988: 414-418; Lin Chin-shui 2003: 347-348; Cheng Mu-chun 2017: 68-72).

    However, later interpretations by PCT leaders and scholars suggested that this declaration marked the beginning of the church‚Äôs pro-independence stance, and the 1978 clarification was merely a necessary concession due to political pressure at the time (Lin Fang-chung 2007: 116).

    Following the 1979 Kaohsiung Incident (Formosa Incident), many PCT pastors, members, and seminary students became actively involved. Kao Chun-ming was arrested and sentenced for sheltering political dissident Shih Ming-teh. This event led to the PCT aligning more closely with the opposition movement, deepening its role in Taiwan‚Äôs democracy and human rights advocacy.

    The Cosmic Light Holistic Care Organization (Cosmic Light Magazine) was founded in September 1973 by a group of overseas Chinese Christian intellectuals. Initially, the publisher was Han Shih-chun, with Liu Yi-ling serving as editor. In 1975, Liu Yi-ling became president, and in 1977, Lin Chi-ping took over the role.

    The magazine‚Äôs first office was a small corner in a traditional Japanese-style house on Xinsheng South Road in Taipei. In 1974, missionary couple Fu Chi-kuan and Hsiu Tse-lan provided a 16-ping (approximately 53 square meters) space in a building on Roosevelt Road to Cosmic Light, charging a symbolic rent of one New Taiwan Dollar while also making donations to support the organization.

    Under Lin Chi-ping‚Äôs leadership, Cosmic Light Magazine evolved into a pre-evangelistic publication, exploring the relationship between Christianity and Chinese culture as well as Chinese church history. It also became a major center for Christian publishing in Taiwan, producing books on these subjects.

    Beyond publishing, Cosmic Light also emphasized counseling and holistic care. In the early 1970s, Lin Chi-ping established ‚ÄúOurs Coffee House‚Äù in an abandoned space at the current site of Taipei Faith Baptist Church (Xin-you Tang). This space was dedicated to youth counseling and care. After the establishment of Cosmic Light Magazine, Ours Coffee House was relocated to its new office on Roosevelt Road.

    Lin Chi-ping believed that conventional psychological counseling overly emphasized scientific professionalism while neglecting the intrinsic value and dignity of life. He argued that it merely helped clients adapt to an empirical, reality-based world without addressing spiritual and existential dimensions. Around 1972, he began advocating a holistic approach to counseling, integrating religious values into the practice.

    In 1975, Cosmic Light Magazine launched a ‚ÄúPsychological Counseling‚Äù column and began planning the Cosmic Light Counseling Center, which later evolved into the Cosmic Light Holistic Care Organization (Lin Chin-shui 2003: 404-407; Hou Ming-chih 2012: 25-33; Lin Chi-ping 2023: 137-142).

    The Translation and Publication of the Today‚Äôs Chinese Version (TCV)

    In 1979, the Bible Society published the Today‚Äôs Chinese Version (TCV), a translation aimed at modern readers, particularly those outside the church, by using contemporary language to make the text more accessible. Beyond its readability and clarity, this translation was significant for fostering cooperation between Catholic and Protestant communities in Taiwan, marking an important step toward Christian unity and reflecting the impact of the Second Vatican Council.

    Before 1970, under the initiative of the Bible Society in Taiwan, Catholic and Protestant representatives had met to discuss the possibility of a joint Chinese translation of the Bible. However, due to disagreements over the standardization of theological terminology, the effort did not advance.

    In October 1971, work on translating and proofreading the Today‚Äôs Chinese Version officially began, led by Xu Mu-shi. The translation team included Luo Wei-ren, Chou Lien-hua, Wang Cheng-chang, and Chiao Ming, while priests such as Liu Xu-tang, Chen Wei-tong, Han Cheng-liang, and Fang Zhi-rong participated in the revision process.

    In 1975, the New Testament was published in two versions‚Äîone for Protestants and one for Catholics‚Äîwith the only differences being terminology:

    • ‚ÄúGod‚Äù (Shangdi, ‰∏äÂ∏ù) was changed to ‚ÄúLord‚Äù (Tianzhu, Â§©‰∏ª) in the Catholic version.

      • ‚ÄúHoly Spirit‚Äù (Shengling, ËÅñÈùà) was changed to ‚ÄúHoly God‚Äù (Shengshen, ËÅñÁ•û).

        • ‚ÄúYahweh‚Äù (Yehehua, ËÄ∂ÂíåËèØ) was changed to ‚ÄúThe Lord‚Äù (Shangzhu, ‰∏ä‰∏ª).

          The Catholic edition was approved for publication by Archbishop Luo Guang of the Taipei Archdiocese, who also wrote its preface.

          In 1979, the complete Bible, including both the Old and New Testaments, was finalized and published. However, the Catholic Church only accepted the earlier New Testament edition, as the full version did not include the Deuterocanonical books (which are part of the Catholic Bible but not the Protestant canon).

          Despite its ecumenical spirit, the translation faced harsh criticism from fundamentalist Protestants, particularly from figures like Wang Yong-xin. They denounced the collaboration between the Bible Society and the Catholic Church as a reflection of ‚Äúmodernist‚Äù (liberal) theology. Wang specifically raised concerns that the translation might include the Deuterocanonical books‚Äîwhich he misleadingly conflated with Apocryphal or non-canonical texts‚Äîthus undermining the authority of the biblical canon (Tseng Ching-pao 2016: 323).

          The controversy surrounding the TCV reflected the political-religious climate of the time, where anti-communist Christian fundamentalists saw opposing Catholic-Protestant unity as part of their mission to defend orthodoxy against ‚Äúmodernist‚Äù and ‚Äúliberal‚Äù influences.

    The Translation and Publication of the ‚ÄúRed-Cover Bible‚Äù

    The Kao-Chen Taiwanese Colloquial Bible (commonly known as the ‚ÄúRed-Cover Bible‚Äù) is the only Taiwanese Hokkien New Testament jointly translated by the Catholic and Protestant churches. It can be regarded as a result of the Church‚Äôs response to the Second Vatican Council‚Äôs promotion of the Christian ecumenical movement. The Second Vatican Council reshaped the relationship between the Catholic Church and other Christian communities, leading to collaborations in various fields, including not only Bible translation but also joint prayers and social services. However, the Catholic Church in Taiwan did not actively respond to this initiative. Its localization efforts were primarily centered on Northern Chinese, neglecting local Minnan and Hakka cultures.

    The Maryknoll Society was arguably the first and most proactive Catholic group to engage with the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan following the Second Vatican Council, largely due to its tradition of preaching in Taiwanese Hokkien. Dissatisfied with merely co-translating the Chinese Bible with Protestants, the Maryknoll Society sought to translate the Bible into Taiwanese Hokkien, resulting in the ‚ÄúRed-Cover Bible.‚Äù This translation adopted the principle of Functional Equivalence and was carried out in two phases.

    The first phase (1965‚Äì1967) involved a team of teachers from the Maryknoll Society‚Äôs language school translating the four Gospels. They then invited four Presbyterian biblical scholars‚ÄîWang Seng-che, Chho√† J√Æn-l√≠, Kuo Tek-liaÃçt, and Tiu‚Åø Tek-hiang‚Äîcommissioned by the Bible Society to assist in proofreading. In the second phase, Maryknoll linguists Philip A. Ho (Koh Kek-hoan) and Bernard W. Kent (T√¢n Pang-t√¨n) collaborated with Presbyterian scholars LoÃçh √õi-j√Æn, Tƒì‚Åø Li√¢n-khun, and Wang Seng-che to translate the remaining 23 books of the New Testament and revise the four Gospels. By September 1972, the Taiwanese Colloquial New Testament was completed, commonly referred to as the ‚ÄúRed-Cover Bible.‚Äù

    The Romanized phonetic system of this translation differs from the Xiamen pronunciation traditionally used by the Presbyterian Church. Instead, it adopts a variant closer to the Zhangzhou dialect, which was prevalent in the Taichung area. This choice was based on the assessment that Zhangzhou-accented Taiwanese had a wider reach and a larger user base at the time.

    Regarding the reconciliation of Catholic and Protestant terminology, the ‚ÄúRed-Cover Bible‚Äù follows Protestant usage by adopting Holy Spirit (ËÅñÈùà) instead of Holy Ghost (ËÅñÁ•û), the latter being the term commonly used by both Catholics and the Presbyterian Church. Additionally, personal and place names are entirely rendered in Protestant transliterations. The term for God integrates elements from both traditions: instead of Tianchu (Â§©‰∏ª) used by Catholics or Shangdi (‰∏äÂ∏ù) preferred by Protestants, the translators combined one character from each to form Shangzhu (‰∏ä‰∏ª).

    Father Albert V. Fedders, one of the key figures in the joint translation, remarked, ‚ÄúFor the first time in Chinese history, Protestants and Catholics patiently and humbly worked together for seven years to complete the Taiwanese Hokkien New Testament‚Äù (Huang Yi-ying 2024: 185-198).

    Indigenous Holy Spirit Revival Movement in the Presbyterian Church

    In 1972, an indigenous Holy Spirit revival movement, similar to the charismatic movement, emerged in the Tayal (Atayal) Presbyterian Church in Tianpu, Jianshi Township, Hsinchu. Through itinerant preaching, it quickly spread to various Tayal communities in Hsinchu, Taoyuan, Yilan, and Taichung. In addition to charismatic manifestations such as prophecy and healing, this revival particularly emphasized repentance, with prophetic messages often focusing on this theme.

    According to a report by Canadian Presbyterian missionary Clare Elliot McGill, women played a dominant role in the movement, leading prayer meetings and organizing itinerant evangelism. The religious phenomena observed in the movement displayed continuity with or syncretism of pre-Christian beliefs. Participants frequently claimed to see visions revealing their own sins or those of others, pronouncing these revelations in a manner akin to divine oracles and commanding repentance. These prophetic rebukes sometimes targeted clergy members, creating tensions with the traditionally male-dominated religious hierarchy. However, the charismatic women involved largely remained compliant with the institutional structure and did not deliberately challenge it.

    The revival‚Äôs emphasis on repentance resonated deeply with the challenges facing the Tayal people at the time, including alcoholism, family disintegration, the erosion of traditional values, and even cases of daughters being sold into prostitution. Simultaneously, women‚Äôs leadership in the movement facilitated a shift in social identity and gender roles, reinforcing the Tayal tradition of valuing female spirituality and elevating the status of female lay leaders within the church.

    Unlike revivals introduced by external Pentecostal or charismatic preachers, this was a self-initiated indigenous revival movement. Clergy members who experienced spiritual renewal through the movement, along with the children of the aforementioned charismatic women, later emerged as leaders of a second wave of Holy Spirit revival among the Tayal in the 1980s. However, this subsequent revival was more influenced by international evangelists and the global Pentecostal or charismatic movement and saw a shift towards male leadership (Shih Su-ying 2015: 128-133).

    The Self-Reliance and Mutual Assistance Movement

    Following the Double Growth Movement, the General Assembly of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) launched the New Century Mission Movement from 1966 to 1971. This initiative was subdivided into the Three H and Four Self Movement (Three H: Cooperation, Mutual Assistance, Training; Four Self: Self-Propagation, Self-Governance, Self-Support, Self-Creation), the Five-Year Plan for Believers‚Äô Training, and the Gospel Outreach Movement. The movement was implemented through five thematic units: Urban Evangelism Year, Rural Evangelism Year, Industrial Evangelism Year, Mountain Evangelism Year, and Overseas Evangelism Year.

    From 1971 to 1975, the PCT launched a new mission initiative, the Faithful Servant Movement, aimed at reflecting on the role of the church. It proposed three fundamental principles: the church‚Äôs mission in the contemporary era, the image of the church as a faithful servant, and the church‚Äôs entrusted responsibility.

    In 1976, the General Assembly integrated these two previous movements into a three-year initiative known as the Self-Reliance and Mutual Assistance Movement.

    The Self-Reliance aspect was divided into faith and economic dimensions.

    • Faith: Since the implementation of the New Century Mission Movement, interdenominational cooperation had influenced the internal structure of the church, leading to a gradual loss of Presbyterian traditions and spirit. In response, the movement set a goal of faith self-reliance, aiming to strengthen the church‚Äôs understanding of its theological heritage.

      • Economics: The movement also addressed the economic disparity between local churches and the wealthier presbyteries and the General Assembly. It sought to restructure the church‚Äôs economic system to achieve financial self-sufficiency.

        The Mutual Assistance aspect emphasized ministry and service, focusing on strengthening unity and collective identity. It encouraged the establishment of sister church relationships between congregations, with the ultimate goal of unifying mountain and lowland churches (Editorial Committee of Understanding the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan 2010: 21-23; Lai Sheng-wen 2012: 17-24).

ÂÄãÂú∞ÊñπÁ∏ΩÈÉ®ÔºåÈÇÑÂª∂‰º∏Ëá≥Ê≠êÊ¥≤„ÄÅÊó•Êú¨„ÄÅÂ∑¥Ë•øÔºåËàáËá∫Áç®ÈÅãÂãï‰∫∫Â£´ÂØÜÂàáÈÖçÂêà„ÄÇ1975Âπ¥ÔºåÊîøÂ∫úÊ≤íÊî∂Ê≥∞ÈõÖË™ûËàáÂè∞Ë™ûÁæÖÈ¶¨Â≠óËÅñÁ∂ìÔºåÂêåÊôÇÂúãÈöõÊÉÖÂã¢ÂèàË∂®Âö¥Â≥ªÔºåÁæéÂúãÁ∏ΩÁµ±Á¶èÁâπË®àÁï´Ë®™Âïè‰∏≠Âúã„ÄÇÂêåÂπ¥Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∏ΩÊúÉ„Äå‰∏ñÁïåÈóú‰øÇÂ∞èÁµÑ„ÄçÂè¨ÈñãÁ¨¨‰∏âÊ¨°„ÄåÊïôÊúÉËàáÁ§æÊúÉ„ÄçÁ†îË®éÊúÉÔºåÂ∞±Áï∂ÊôÇÂúãÂÆ∂ËôïÂ¢ÉÈÄ≤Ë°åÁ†îË®éÔºåËàáÊúÉËÄÖÊèêÂá∫„ÄàÊàëÂÄëÁöÑÂëºÁ±≤„ÄâÔºåÂëºÁ±≤ÊîøÂ∫úÂ∞äÈáçÂÆóÊïôËá™Áî±‰ª•ÂèäÁôºÈÇÑÊñ∞Ë≠ØÁôΩË©±Â≠óËÅñÁ∂ìÔºå‰∏¶Ë¶ÅÊ±ÇÂáÜË®±ÁπºÁ∫åÂá∫Áâà‰ªª‰ΩïË™ûË®Ä‰πãËÅñÁ∂ì„ÄÇÂêåÊôÇÔºåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ‰∫¶ÂëºÁ±≤ÊîøÂ∫úËÆìÊïôÊúÉÂä†ÂÖ•ÂúãÈöõÊÄßÊïôÊúÉÁµÑÁπîÔºå‰ª•Ë£úÊïëËøëÂπ¥‰æÜËá∫ÁÅ£Âú®ÂúãÈöõÊîøÊ≤ªÂú∞‰ΩçÁöÑÂç±Ê©ü„ÄÇÂÖ®ÊñáÊñº9Êúà28Êó•ÂàäÁôªÊñº„ÄäËá∫ÁÅ£ÊïôÊúÉÂÖ¨Â†±„ÄãÔºå11Êúà18Êó•Á∏ΩÊúÉÈÄöÈÅé‰ª•Êú¨ÂÆ£Ë®ÄÁÇ∫Ë©≤ÊúÉÁ´ãÂ†¥„ÄÇ1977Âπ¥8Êúà16Êó•ÔºåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÂèàÁôºË°®„ÄàÂè∞ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ‰∫∫Ê¨äÂÆ£Ë®Ä„ÄâÔºåÂÄ°Ë®Ä„ÄåÂè∞ÁÅ£ÁöÑÂ∞á‰æÜÊáâÁî±Âè∞ÁÅ£‰∏ÄÂçÉ‰∏ÉÁôæËê¨‰ΩèÊ∞ëÊ±∫ÂÆö„ÄçÔºå‰∏¶Âá∫Áèæ„Äå‰ΩøÂè∞ÁÅ£ÊàêÁÇ∫‰∏ÄÂÄãÊñ∞ËÄåÁç®Á´ãÁöÑÂúãÂÆ∂„ÄçÁöÑË®¥Ê±Ç„ÄÇÂõ†1976Âπ¥ÁæéÂúãÁ∏ΩÁµ±Âç°Áâπ‰∏ªÂºµ‰ª•„Äå‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄçÁÇ∫Â§ñ‰∫§ÊîøÁ≠ñÊ∫ñÂâáÔºåËÄå‰∏≠ÂúãÊîøÊ¨äÂèàÊèöË®Ä‰ª•Ê≠¶Âäõ‰ΩµÂêûËá∫ÁÅ£ÔºåÈï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉ‰ª•Âç°ÁâπÁöÑ„Äå‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄçÊîøÁ≠ñÂàáÂÖ•ÔºåÊèêÈÜíÁæéÂúãÂç°ÁâπÂàáÂãøÂá∫Ë≥£Âè∞ÁÅ£‰∏ÄÂçÉ‰∏ÉÁôæËê¨ÁôæÂßìÁöÑ‰∫∫Ê¨ä„ÄÇÈÄô‰∏âÂ§ßÂÆ£Ë®ÄÂºïËµ∑ÊîøÂ∫úÂº∑ÁÉàÁöÑÈúáÊíºËàá‰∏çÊªøÔºåÈõôÊñπÁöÑÊîøÊïôÈóú‰øÇÊó•Ë∂®Á∑äÂºµÔºàÈªÉÂÖãÂÖà2021Ôºö25ÔºõË™çË≠òËá∫ÁÅ£Âü∫Áù£Èï∑ËÄÅÊïôÊúÉÁ∑®ËºØÂ∞èÁµÑ2010Ôºö25-28Ôºâ„ÄÇ

        The Three Major Declarations of the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan

        In response to United Nations Resolution 2758, passed on October 25, 1971, which recognized the People‚Äôs Republic of China as ‚Äúthe only legitimate representative of China‚Äù and expelled Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs representatives from the UN, coupled with U.S. President Richard Nixon‚Äôs announcement of his planned visit to China, the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) issued the Statement on National Affairs on December 29, 1971.

        The draft of this statement was initially proposed by the Churches‚Äô Cooperation Association. However, as it failed to gain support from other denominations and churches‚Äîmany of which refused to sign and subsequently withdrew‚Äîthe PCT ultimately released the statement independently. It consisted of two main parts:

        1. To the international community, the PCT expressed its aspiration to live in peace, freedom, and justice, while firmly rejecting Communist rule.

          1. To the domestic government, the PCT called for internal reforms to safeguard Taiwan‚Äôs international standing. The statement emphasized the need for comprehensive re-elections of central representatives within the ‚Äúfree region‚Äù (Taiwan, Penghu, Kinmen, and Matsu) before any potential national reunification.

            At the time, prominent PCT leaders, such as Huang Chang-hui and Huang Wu-tung, who had been living abroad due to the ecumenical movement controversies of the 1960s, launched the Taiwanese Self-Determination Movement in response to the PCT‚Äôs declaration. This movement started in the United States and established nearly 30 local chapters across North America, later expanding to Europe, Japan, and Brazil, working closely with Taiwanese independence activists.

            In 1975, the government confiscated the Atayal and Taiwanese Romanized Bibles while international pressures intensified, as U.S. President Gerald Ford planned a visit to China. That same year, the PCT‚Äôs World Affairs Committee convened its third Church and Society conference to discuss Taiwan‚Äôs political situation. Attendees drafted Our Appeal, urging the government to respect religious freedom, return the confiscated newly translated Romanized Bibles, and allow the continued publication of Bibles in any language. Additionally, the PCT called on the government to permit the church to join international Christian organizations to mitigate Taiwan‚Äôs deteriorating international standing. The full text of this appeal was published in the Taiwan Church News on September 28, and on November 18, the General Assembly officially adopted it as the church‚Äôs position.

            On August 16, 1977, the PCT issued the Declaration on Human Rights, asserting that ‚Äúthe future of Taiwan should be determined by its 17 million residents‚Äù and introducing the demand for ‚ÄúTaiwan to become a new and independent nation.‚Äù Against the backdrop of U.S. President Jimmy Carter‚Äôs human rights diplomacy in 1976 and China‚Äôs threats of military invasion against Taiwan, the PCT leveraged Carter‚Äôs policies to urge the U.S. government not to compromise the human rights of Taiwan‚Äôs 17 million people.

            These three major declarations provoked a strong reaction from the Taiwanese government, escalating tensions between the state and the church (Huang Kexian 2021: 25; Editorial Committee of Understanding the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan 2010: 25-28).

            Indigenous Theology

            In 1978, Wang Hsien-chih proposed the concept of Indigenous Theology (ÈÑâÂúüÁ•ûÂ≠∏) at the alumni seminar of Tainan Theological College and Seminary. The term was formally introduced in September 1979 at a meeting of the Asian Christian Conference‚Äôs Theology Committee in Taipei to represent Taiwan‚Äôs contextual theology at the time.

            During the 1970s, a group of Taiwanese cultural figures sought to frame Taiwanese national consciousness as a key discourse in nativist literature (ÈÑâÂúüÊñáÂ≠∏). The idea of Indigenous Theology reflected this historical context. Wang Hsien-chih argued that Indigenous Theology was an effort to construct a theological framework that both identified with and affirmed Taiwan. Using the method of contextual theology, he attempted to develop a uniquely Taiwanese theological path by drawing insights from both Taiwan‚Äôs history and the Bible.

            Wang‚Äôs Indigenous Theology incorporated elements similar to Shoki Coe‚Äôs theology of displacement, likening the experience of Taiwanese people crossing the Black Water Channel (Taiwan Strait) to that of the Israelites crossing the Red Sea. He depicted both groups as God‚Äôs chosen people, called to journey toward the Promised Land in pursuit of freedom. He also drew parallels between Taiwan‚Äôs political situation and the biblical narrative of the Israelites‚Äô Exodus, nation-building, exile, and restoration.

            Wang defined ‚Äú‰π°‚Äù (Xiang) as homeland or people and ‚ÄúÂúü‚Äù (Tu) as land or natural resources. He argued that when homeland and land are combined with sovereignty, a nation is formed. Within this framework, Christianity promotes a ‚Äúpolitics of indigenous identity‚Äù (ÈÑâÂúüÊîøÊ≤ª), where the people serve as the‰∏ª‰Ωì (central subjects), and the sovereignty granted by God enables them to choose their own government. Thus, the core themes of Indigenous Theology are people, land, power, and God, which Wang interpreted according to Taiwan‚Äôs political context in the 1970s:

            1. People (‰∫∫Ê∞ë): Under the Kuomintang‚Äôs authoritarian rule, Taiwanese people had lost their dignity and humanity. Wang called for them to become ‚Äúnew creations in Christ‚Äù, receiving grace and courage from God to achieve liberation.

              1. Land (ÂúüÂú∞): He argued that Taiwanese people had the right to determine the future of their land while welcoming people of all ethnic backgrounds to contribute to its development. He emphasized that land was the key distinction between Taiwanese Indigenous Theology and Latin American Liberation Theology.

                1. Power (Ê¨äÂäõ): Wang stressed that Taiwanese people must learn from history to prevent the corruption of power. In particular, under foreign rule, it was crucial to theologically analyze the nature, structure, and application of power.

                  1. God (‰∏äÂ∏ù): Indigenous Theology critiques deification, especially the sanctification of political leaders or the idolization of power.

                    Essentially, Indigenous Theology was deeply rooted in Taiwan‚Äôs contemporary reality, especially concerning the nation‚Äôs future and self-identity. While it emphasized ‚Äúhomeland‚Äù and ‚Äúland‚Äù, it also addressed political concerns and ideological formation, making it a form of Taiwanese contextual liberation theology. The affirmation of Taiwan‚Äôs indigenous identity was fundamentally shaped by the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan‚Äôs tense relationship with Chiang Kai-shek‚Äôs regime and its pan-Chinese nationalist ideology, the people‚Äôs anti-colonial resistance movement, and the broader atmosphere of post-martial law Taiwan (Chiu Kai-li 2021: 131).

                    Contextual Theology

                    In 1973, during his tenure at the Theological Education Fund of the World Council of Churches, Huang Chang-hui, together with Aharon Sapsezian, introduced the concept of contextualization in theology (Á•ûÂ≠∏ËôïÂ¢ÉÂåñ). Huang argued that churches in the Third World must first decontextualize Western theology and then recontextualize faith and theology within their own socio-historical realities to establish the theological subjectivity of the Third World.

                    Huang described contextualization as a theological method that involves a continuous interaction between the text of Christian Scripture and the socio-political context, generating new biblical interpretations. He asserted that authentic theological reflection can only emerge within a specific context, emphasizing that all theology is contextual theology. Christians must critically interpret their social and political environment, discern the signs of the times, and engage in theological reflection that directly responds to their lived realities.

                    Understanding Christian theology as contextualized means taking seriously the contextual dimensions of the Gospel and responding with critical theological engagement. Huang argued that contextual theology should replace indigenization theology (Êú¨Ëâ≤ÂåñÁ•ûÂ≠∏), as the latter primarily focuses on cultural adaptation and assimilation while neglecting the broader social, historical, and political dimensions that shape theological development.

                    For Huang, contextualization was deeply concerned with the secular, technological, and economic transformations of the modern world, as well as the struggles for justice‚Äîelements that characterized the historical trajectory of many Third World nations. Contextual theology, therefore, actively responds to social and cultural changes rather than merely engaging with traditional cultural preservation.

                    Huang also warned against false contextualization, which he defined as blind adaptation that turns into cultural religiosity. In contrast, authentic contextualization is prophetic, arising from a genuine encounter between God‚Äôs Word and the realities of the world. True contextual theology is deeply rooted in and committed to a specific historical context, striving to challenge and transform the existing social and political structures.

                    In the 1970s, contextual theology in Taiwan facilitated theological reflections that engaged with Taiwan‚Äôs socio-political realities, including the rise of Taiwanese consciousness, critiques of the Kuomintang regime, and discussions of Taiwanese independence. Additionally, contextual theology contributed to the development of Asian theology, significantly influencing contemporary Christian thought (Chiu Kai-li 2021: 131; Zhuo Xin-ping 2007: 224; Kwan 2014: 41; Pears 2010: 23).

                    Chen Gu-ying (July 1, 1935‚Äì )

                    Chen Gu-ying is a Taiwanese philosopher born in Changting, Fujian. In 1949, he moved to Taiwan. He obtained a master‚Äôs degree in philosophy from National Taiwan University (NTU) in 1964, studying under Yin Haiguang and Fang Dongmei. After graduation, he taught at the NTU Department of Philosophy, eventually becoming an associate professor.

                    Chen, along with Wang Xiaobo, contributed political commentaries to University Magazine, advocating liberalism and promoting the Protect Diaoyutai Movement, encouraging university students to participate. At the time, the government, fearing potential unrest from student movements‚Äîparticularly concerns that Chinese nationalist sentiments within the movement could lead to collusion with the Chinese Communist Party‚Äîsuppressed the movement under the pretext of ‚Äúanti-communism.‚Äù This led to a purge of liberal scholars in NTU‚Äôs Philosophy Department, an incident known as the NTU Philosophy Department Affair. In 1973, Chen and Wang were detained by the Garrison Command on charges of ‚Äúpropaganda for the enemy‚Äù and were subsequently dismissed from their positions. After martial law was lifted, the case was overturned, and in 1997, Chen was reinstated as a professor at NTU. He is currently a Distinguished Chair Professor in Humanities at the Department of Philosophy, Peking University (Chen Shichang 2015: 107-108).

                    Between 1968 and the 1970s, Chen engaged in polemics with several Christian scholars and clergy, including Tang Youzhi and Wang Jinghong. He based his arguments on humanism and Eastern humanistic thought, interpreting the Bible literally and criticizing it for containing irrational accounts that contradicted common ethical understandings. He argued that the God of the Bible was autocratic, arbitrary, and bloodthirsty.

                    Chen asserted that God is a human creation, existing to serve human needs. Thus, if God is a projection of human ideals, he should embody benevolence and other fundamental virtues. Any deity that acts contrary to moral principles should be universally rejected. He further argued that God‚Äôs love is conditional, requiring humans to love God first in order to be loved in return. In other words, only by becoming God‚Äôs servants can people receive rewards, which contradicts the notion of Christian universal love.

                    His critiques drew responses from various Christian writers, but Chen refrained from further engagement. He believed that most rebuttals came from a faith-based perspective rather than scholarly theological discourse, rendering the debate unproductive. Moreover, at the time, his primary academic focus was on Zhuangzi studies, leaving him no time for further theological debate (University Magazine Editorial Committee 1970: 1-13, 141-142; Anonymous 2011).

                    Christianity was never a primary area of Chen‚Äôs academic interest, and his critiques demonstrated a limited scholarly understanding of Christian theology. Nevertheless, his arguments provoked significant discussions, prompting Christian intellectuals to reconsider how to engage with contemporary philosophical and intellectual discourse.

                    The Republic of China (ROC) Regime

                    Amid the anti-communist and Christian apologetic atmosphere of the 1960s, and with the global rise of fundamentalist groups such as the International Council of Christian Churches aligning with anti-communist forces, the World Council of Churches (WCC) was portrayed as an organization that had abandoned pure faith, was pro-communist or tolerant of communism, and was suspected of communist infiltration. Under government pressure, the Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) was forced to withdraw from the WCC in 1970.

                    To dispel government suspicions of pro-communist affiliations, the PCT established the Anti-Communist Implementation Committee (dissolved in 1973). Throughout 1970, presbyteries across Taiwan organized a series of anti-communist lectures, actively demonstrating their opposition to communism. Even after leaving the WCC, the committee continued hosting anti-communist gatherings. In 1972, it issued a directive urging all congregations to fully support and participate in the Second World Christian Anti-Communist Congress. Furthermore, the Statement on National Affairs issued by the PCT in 1971 explicitly reaffirmed an anti-communist stance, conveying a non-compromising attitude toward communism to international organizations and the U.S. government. However, these efforts failed to gain recognition from the ROC government.

                    One of the statement‚Äôs co-authors, missionary Milton D. Meder, was expelled from Taiwan on March 5, 1972. From the time of the first declaration‚Äôs release, the Kuomintang (KMT) security apparatus engaged in surveillance, wiretapping, and intimidation of the PCT, even interfering in church meetings and discussions. In 1975, the government confiscated Bibles and hymnals in Indigenous languages as well as Taiwanese Romanized Bibles, intensifying its suppression of religious freedom and local culture. In response, the PCT issued Our Appeal, urging the government to respect religious freedom and eliminate its distrust of the church.

                    By 1977, amid shifting international dynamics, the PCT released the Declaration on Human Rights, which explicitly called for a ‚Äúnew and independent nation.‚Äù This statement further enraged the KMT regime, which banned the corresponding issue of the Taiwan Church News and intensified surveillance of PCT leadership.

                    The three major declarations issued by the PCT in the 1970s directly challenged the ruling authority. Overseas activities of PCT leaders, as well as their interactions with foreign individuals and groups, were closely monitored by the KMT‚Äôs intelligence agencies. The primary aim was to isolate the church from connections with overseas dissidents and Taiwanese independence movements that opposed the KMT regime. However, the PCT refused to submit. It continued engaging with overseas dissident groups and issued statements within Taiwan to counter the government‚Äôs unjust actions. Skillfully leveraging its transnational religious networks, the PCT mobilized international pressure against the KMT regime. From this point forward, the church increasingly took on a confrontational stance against the government (Tseng Ching-pao 2016: 194-217; Chiu Kai-li 2021: 165-167; Huang Kexian 2021: 25-26).

                      Confiscation of the Romanized Bible

                      In 1974, during a Sunday service at Bo‚Äôai Presbyterian Church in Heping Township, Taichung County (an Atayal congregation), the police entered the church and forcibly seized Atayal-language Bibles and hymnals. On January 15, 1975, the government, citing violations of the Mandarin Language Policy, confiscated 1,638 newly printed copies of the Newly Translated Taiwanese Romanized New Testament (Red-Cover Bible) and 655 reprinted copies of the Barker Colloquial Taiwanese Bible, published by the Bible Society in Taiwan.

                      News of the confiscation quickly spread internationally, drawing coverage from foreign media. On May 31, the Ministry of Education issued an official statement justifying the confiscation on the grounds of language policy violations, insisting that ‚Äúthe Chinese government has no intention of interfering with or restricting religious freedom‚Äù and that ‚Äúthe ban was solely due to language policy violations.‚Äù

                      On June 2, Bible Society Director Chho√† J√Æn-l√≠ submitted a petition to the Ministry of Education requesting the return of the confiscated Barker Colloquial Taiwanese Bible. In mid-June, the government agreed to return and allow the sale of these copies. However, it upheld its decision to continue banning the distribution of the newly printed Red-Cover Bible, stating that ‚Äúsuch publications shall not be issued or circulated.‚Äù

                      Meanwhile, Rev. Lester Kim, chairman of the Asian Presbyterian Caucus of the United Presbyterian Church in the USA, wrote to ROC President Yen Chia-kan, expressing strong dissatisfaction over the government‚Äôs confiscation of the Taiwanese Romanized Bible, denouncing it as a deprivation of the Taiwanese people‚Äôs right to read the Bible in their native language.

                      The Presbyterian Church in Taiwan (PCT) also condemned the government‚Äôs selective return of the Barker Colloquial Bible while continuing to ban the Romanized Red-Cover Bible. In response, the PCT issued the declaration Our Appeal later that year. The first point of the declaration‚Äî‚ÄúSafeguarding the constitutional right to religious freedom‚Äù‚Äîwas explicitly directed at the government‚Äôs confiscation of the Bible. The declaration urged the government to uphold the constitutional guarantee of religious freedom, return the newly translated Romanized Bible, and permit the continued publication of Bibles in any language.

                      On November 18, the PCT General Assembly‚Äôs Standing Committee formally adopted Our Appeal as the church‚Äôs official stance. The committee instructed the Bible Society and church leadership to continue negotiations with the government for the return of the remaining confiscated Bibles. However, the authorities refused to comply.

                      Interestingly, the government showed greater leniency toward the Catholic Church, permitting it to use the newly translated Romanized Red-Cover Bible for missionary purposes. This discrepancy may have been due to the fact that Catholic believers were less reliant on personal Bible reading compared to Protestants, and the Catholic Church maintained a better relationship with the government (Huang Yi-ying 2024: 188-205).

Christian or Pro-Christian Events/Movements
